    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }

      AppleID.auth.init({

<div id="descriptionContainer">
  
      <div id="description" class="readable stacked" style="right:0">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer13621493747234859717">Almost Transparent Blue is a brutal tale of lost youth in a Japanese port town close to an American military base. Murakami's image-intensive narrative paints a portrait of a group of friends locked in a destructive cycle of sex, drugs and rock'n'roll. The novel is all but plotless, but the raw and often violent prose takes us on a rollercoaster ride through reality and ha</span>
  <span id="freeText13621493747234859717" style="display:none">Almost Transparent Blue is a brutal tale of lost youth in a Japanese port town close to an American military base. Murakami's image-intensive narrative paints a portrait of a group of friends locked in a destructive cycle of sex, drugs and rock'n'roll. The novel is all but plotless, but the raw and often violent prose takes us on a rollercoaster ride through reality and hallucination, highs and lows, in which the characters and their experiences come vividly to life. Trapped in passivity, they gain neither passion nor pleasure from their adventures. Yet out of the alienation, boredom and underlying rage and grief emerges a strangely quiet and almost equally shocking beauty. Ryu Murakami's first novel, Almost Transparent Blue won the coveted Akutagawa literary prize and became an instant bestseller. Representing a sharp and conscious turning away from the introspective trend of postwar Japanese literature, it polarized critics and public alike and soon attracted international attention as an alternative view of modern Japan.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13621493747234859717" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

      </div>

    <div class="section firstReview">
      

<div id="review_379609846" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/379609846" />
    <a title="Jeffrey Keeten" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3427339-jeffrey-keeten"><img alt="Jeffrey Keeten" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1301087352p2/3427339.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/379609846?book_show_action=true">Jul 29, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jeffrey Keeten" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jeffrey Keeten" href="/user/show/3427339-jeffrey-keeten">Jeffrey Keeten</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3427339-jeffrey-keeten?shelf=the-japanese">the-japanese</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer379609846" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2808220612469458310"><b>"I put the thin fragment of glass, dripping blood, in my pocket, and ran out into the misty road. The doors and windows of the houses were shut, nothing was moving. I thought I'd been swallowed by a huge living thing, that I was turning around and around in its stomach like the hero of some fairy tale."</b><br/><br/><a href='https://s1183.photobucket.com/albums/x462/jkeeten/?action=view&current=AlmostTransparentBlue-1.jpg' target='_blank' rel='nofollow noopener'>
  <img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1535315849i/26204542.jpg' alt='AlmostTransparentBlue' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/>
</a><br/><b>Almost Transparent Blue</b><br/><br/>A warning to any potential readers of this book. There is explicit, graphic sex in the first half of this novel. If you are prudish about group sex, alternative sex, or </span>
  <span id="freeText2808220612469458310" style="display:none"><b>"I put the thin fragment of glass, dripping blood, in my pocket, and ran out into the misty road. The doors and windows of the houses were shut, nothing was moving. I thought I'd been swallowed by a huge living thing, that I was turning around and around in its stomach like the hero of some fairy tale."</b><br><br><a href="https://s1183.photobucket.com/albums/x462/jkeeten/?action=view&amp;current=AlmostTransparentBlue-1.jpg" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1535315849i/26204542.jpg" alt="AlmostTransparentBlue" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
</a><br><b>Almost Transparent Blue</b><br><br>A warning to any potential readers of this book. There is explicit, graphic sex in the first half of this novel. If you are prudish about group sex, alternative sex, or say sex involving a foot that will be seared into your memory for the rest of your life you should avoid this novel. If you have issues with rampant drug use and drug/alcohol induced vomiting you should avoid this novel. <br><br>The first cool thing about this novel is the fact that it was published in 1976 almost a full decade before <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/2751.Bret_Easton_Ellis" title="Bret Easton Ellis" rel="nofollow noopener">Bret Easton Ellis</a>came up out of his own drug induced haze to write the novel <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/9915.Less_Than_Zero" title="Less Than Zero by Bret Easton Ellis" rel="nofollow noopener">Less Than Zero</a>. I read Ellis and I read <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/86147.Bright_Lights__Big_City" title="Bright Lights, Big City by Jay McInerney" rel="nofollow noopener">Bright Lights, Big City</a>, and later <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/29044.The_Secret_History" title="The Secret History by Donna Tartt" rel="nofollow noopener">The Secret History</a>and enjoyed them to varying degrees. I'd heard of the other Murakami, but his books were somewhat elusive to me in the 1980s. So finally here in 2012 I read this book and discover that if I had read this book back in 1985 I would have laughed at the feeble attempts of the "brat pack" to write "edgy" novels. <br><br>I wonder did Ellis read this book before he wrote <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/9915.Less_Than_Zero" title="Less Than Zero by Bret Easton Ellis" rel="nofollow noopener">Less Than Zero</a>? <br><br>This book kicks you in the nuts and as your falling to the ground you catch a knee to the chin that stands you back up so Murakami can slug you a few more times before letting you fall into bloody heap wishing you could reach that syringe full of smack just beyond your blood dripping reach. <br><br>Our characters get into an altercation with a security guard. They break his arm, but they don't stop there. By the time they are done he is a mess.<br><br><i>The blood smeared and dripping over the lower half of his face was a black mask. The veins in his forehead bulging, he tried to pull himself along by his elbows. Perhaps seized by some fresh pain, he mumbled, lay on his side, his feet trembling. His vomit-covered belly heaved up and down.</i><br><br>There is a lack of humanity running through this novel. The main characters are horrible to everyone including each other. Ryu, the main character and also the stand in for the author, does show some real tenderness towards his friends, but not without some sensual benefit to himself. There is a lack of soul, not in the religious sense because that is all silly nonsense (as if my life force can be bartered with), but in the lack of substance in these characters. <br><br>I read a book several years ago, part of a trilogy of which only two parts have been published, by a guy named <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/270652.James_A__Mangum" title="James A. Mangum" rel="nofollow noopener">James A. Mangum</a>. In one of the two books, memory escapes me, he talks about these "soulless" creatures walking the Earth, the offspring of "Angels" and human females. They lack a soul because they are not exactly human, but they are able to assimilate with their soul carrying brethren. They lack that important part of humanity that allows us to really care about each other. <b>I think we've all met people that resemble that description.</b> The really scary part about accepting any of this as potentially true is that as these soulless creatures mate with other humans their offspring is also soulless. With each generation more humanity is lost.<br><br>I read <i>Less than Zero</i> for a second time a couple of years ago before I read <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/7519866.Imperial_Bedrooms_" title="Imperial Bedrooms  by Bret Easton Ellis" rel="nofollow noopener">Imperial Bedrooms</a>and I thought about the Mangum concept then as well. There is something missing in these characters and of course the drug use, the promiscuous sex, random violence, alcohol induced vomiting is all an attempt to feel something, anything. <br><br>The crew goes to party with some American black men from the military base that is near where they live. Ryu is sharing a description of the scene.<br><br><i>I was completely stoned. I felt as if my insides were oozing out through every pore, and other people's sweat and breath were flowing in. Especially the lower half of my body felt heavy and sore, as if sunk into thick mud, and my mouth itched to hold somebody's prick and drain it. While we ate the fruit piled on plates and drank wine, the whole room was raped with heat. I wanted my skin peeled off. I wanted to take in the greased, shiny bodies of the black men and rock them inside me. Cherry cheesecake, grapes in black hands, steaming boiled crab legs breaking with a snap, clear sweet pale purple American wine, pickles like dead men's wart-covered fingers, bacon sandwiches like the mouths of women, salad dripping pink mayonnaise.</i><br><br>There are cockroaches that spew different colors when crushed. There is a pet rabbitcide. There is a doctor that explains to Yoshiyama, being treated for a suicide attempt, the absolute best way to kill himself next time. They ingest mescaline, acid, heroin, Hyminol, Nibrole, glue, marijuana, and something called a Crystal Ship. They don't want you looking at them, but they do everything they can to insure that you are watching.<br><br>Even moments of beauty are seen with jaded eyes. <br><br><i>At the edge of the wide grounds was a pool, and around it flowers were planted. Like the eruptions on a rotting corpse, like a serum with multiplying cancer cells, the flowers were blooming. Against the background of a wall that rippled like white cloth, they scattered on the ground or suddenly danced up in the wind. I'm cold, as if I were dead.</i><br><br><a href="https://s1183.photobucket.com/albums/x462/jkeeten/?action=view&amp;current=RyuMurakami-1.jpg" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1535315849i/26204541.jpg" alt="RyuMurakami" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
</a><br><b>The Other Murakami</b><br><br>I was uncertain of this book for the first fifty pages. If I were one of those reviewers that reviews books that they don't finish I would have given it two stars, but as the book moves forward I started to begrudgingly change my mind about the book. It is hard to adjust first impressions, but the accumulation of stark images started to impress me. You may toss this book across the room a few times (hopefully not the first edition hardcover as it is becoming rather rare), but let it set for a few minutes, a few hours, or a few days and then pick it back up again. This book broke ground and the imitators that came later don't measure up. <br><br>If you wish to see more of my most recent book and movie reviews, visit <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://www.jeffreykeeten.com">http://www.jeffreykeeten.com</a><br>I also have a Facebook blogger page at:<a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.facebook.com/JeffreyKeeten">https://www.facebook.com/JeffreyKeeten</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="2808220612469458310" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1728175482" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1728175482" />
    <a title="Jr Bacdayan" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/16587936-jr-bacdayan"><img alt="Jr Bacdayan" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1552066595p2/16587936.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1728175482?book_show_action=true">Aug 14, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jr Bacdayan" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jr Bacdayan" href="/user/show/16587936-jr-bacdayan">Jr Bacdayan</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1728175482" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8509956785894880400">There is a shrill noise often lost in the background of the more important noises in the world. This piercing cry found abundantly around no matter where you go like pigeons flocking empty streets at the break of dawn, for whatever reason, seems to be relegated as insignificant and unworthy of the spotlight and stages of the world’s short attention span. Many names have been given to this jarring sound, but for the purposes of this cajolery or whatever this is, let us call it the reckless abando</span>
  <span id="freeText8509956785894880400" style="display:none">There is a shrill noise often lost in the background of the more important noises in the world. This piercing cry found abundantly around no matter where you go like pigeons flocking empty streets at the break of dawn, for whatever reason, seems to be relegated as insignificant and unworthy of the spotlight and stages of the world’s short attention span. Many names have been given to this jarring sound, but for the purposes of this cajolery or whatever this is, let us call it the reckless abandon of youth. The accumulation of mistakes, the period to be reckless and stupid, to burn out, be wild, to explore the often dissonant and sometimes psychedelic essence of youngness, free from responsibilities and pretensions. Perhaps it is the most beautiful moment in life, or perhaps it might be a dark nightmare to be a spurned and forgotten? <br /><br />When I was a bit younger I had my fair share of teenage angst that led me to a path of abandon, partly because of hubris, I wanted to have experienced life and all it had to offer me if I wanted to write something that would matter, because somewhere I’d read that a writer is only the sum of his experiences, and partly because I craved the thrill. Those days are over, but there are days when I still feel the call of youth. Days when I would look at the sky and think ‘Is this it?’ Days when I’d want to forget everything. They don’t come often, but when they come, I feel like I’m being gripped by the past, gripped by a part of myself slowly fading away and I get this bitter taste in my mouth. <br /><br />Almost Transparent Blue is a novella about this ragtag, exhilarating time of life filled with anger, confusion, and fear. Ryu Murakami takes the shrill noise and puts it into a record and plays it, only he amplifies it and puts it in the highest volume possible that your eardrums could burst. The vibration of the speakers working on their threshold imitating an earthquake keeps you on the edge and awake. As you might have suspected, it is very raunchy and somewhat episodic. Moments of drug induced euphoria, drunken revelries, to descriptive orgies and dark reflections fill the pages of this colorful book. And from the all of the colors of a psychedelic rainbow, to all the shades of intense darkness at the heart of an empty soul, surprisingly, a feeling of peace stems out. And this feeling of peace has the hue of an almost transparent blue. <br /><br />This is a rewarding book, but at times it was painful to read. Not because the scenes were too much for me, but rather it reminded me too much of a part of myself I am no longer in touch with, a past that I do not regret, but a past that sometimes I want forgotten.</span>
  <a data-text-id="8509956785894880400" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_2285125382" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2285125382" />
    <a title="Daniel Clausen" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/957634-daniel-clausen"><img alt="Daniel Clausen" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1603533755p2/957634.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2285125382?book_show_action=true">Feb 06, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Daniel Clausen" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Daniel Clausen" href="/user/show/957634-daniel-clausen">Daniel Clausen</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/957634-daniel-clausen?shelf=books-of-2018">books-of-2018</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2285125382" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer463458681778204722">Aw, the silly depravity of youth. How do I count the ways? <br/><br/>What is it to read this book? To imagine a decadence I never experienced...to be young and frivolous, to exchange idealism with nihilism so that I can make my depravity a pool of vomit to swim in. <br/><br/>As I reach out to grab the syringe I've never used to shoot up once more words that aren't heroin, I think about my 17-year-old self. He's reading "Trainspotting" in a place that doesn't feel like home, wondering if he can ever go home. He imag</span>
  <span id="freeText463458681778204722" style="display:none">Aw, the silly depravity of youth. How do I count the ways? <br /><br />What is it to read this book? To imagine a decadence I never experienced...to be young and frivolous, to exchange idealism with nihilism so that I can make my depravity a pool of vomit to swim in. <br /><br />As I reach out to grab the syringe I've never used to shoot up once more words that aren't heroin, I think about my 17-year-old self. He's reading "Trainspotting" in a place that doesn't feel like home, wondering if he can ever go home. He images a 16-year-old self. He's reading "A Clockwork Orange", his eyes peeled open by mechanical claws.<br /><br />I bring out those mechanical claws so that I can see once again the literary uses of bodily juices -- semen, vomit, blood, and bile of different colors, purple, brown, green...and Almost Transparent Blue? I'm not sure bile comes in that color. <br /><br />In order to get to great moments of beauty, we have to be presented with the bile of Mr. Murakami Ryu's life....a real life? This wasn't fiction? Was "Trainspotting" fiction? Is this book review fiction? <br /><br />Ryu Murakami writes:<br />"I took a fragment of glass about the size of my thumbnail out of my pocket and wiped the blood off it. The little fragment with its smooth hollow reflected the brightening sky. Under the sky stretched the hospital and far away the tree-lined street and the town. The horizon of the shadowy reflected town made a delicate curving line. Its curves were the same, the same as the time I'd almost killed Lilly on the runway in the rain, that white curved line that burned for an instant with the thunder. Like the wave-filled foggy horizon of the sea, like a woman's white arm, a gentle curve. All the time, since I didn't know when I'd been surrounded by this whitish curving. The fragment of glass with the blood on its edge, as it soaked up the dawn air, was almost transparent. It was a boundless blue, almost transparent." <br /><br /><br />I shouldn't have typed out those lines. You didn't work to get there. You didn't have to read about semen and bile on the way to work. You didn't have to look on as a security guard was beat half to death and girl was almost beat half to death, and the sex and the orgies, not really erotica -- there was nothing sexy about those orgies, just the absurd, awful, depraved depravity of...aw, decadent youth. You reach for a syringe and it's Ryu Murakami's youthful literary exploits, or it's "Trainspotting", or it's "A Clockwork Orange". <br /><br />Someone said that this was a stream of consciousness...I doubt that this was a stream of consciousness. It seemed more to me like a book. It felt like a book. It could be a stream of consciousness. But I think it was a book. It could have been a diary, it could have been the sound of an airplane or a fly buzzing behind my ear. It could have been a Sunday in May with me staring at a coffee shop worker. She's wondering what I'm doing...I'm a fly buzzing behind your ear, ma'am. I'm a fly trying to remember what it's like to be a 17-year-old contemplating the Almost Transparent Blue of love...I just hope that I can get through the other shades of bile to get there.<br /><br />I reach for a syringe with the latest drug of choice and find this book. I try to turn its pages and it turns me blue. </span>
  <a data-text-id="463458681778204722" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <a title="Greg" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/42508-greg"><img alt="Greg" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1368721201p2/42508.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/117856672?book_show_action=true">Aug 20, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Greg" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Greg" href="/user/show/42508-greg">Greg</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer117856672" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6410293289309405327"><a href='http://www.youtube.com/watch?v=bHVDibABUGk' rel='nofollow noopener'>
  <b>(Soundtrack for this review)</b>
</a><br/><br/>Do you want to know what I don't give a shit about? This book. <br/><br/>I made it a little more than a quarter through it (35 pages to be exact). I found the writing to be blah (to be fair, I think it is the translation), the type to be a bit too small and the font to be faintly illegible. I dreaded having to go on break and read more of this book, and did internal backflips when I noticed the a new Harpers just went on sale before I went on break and saw there was a new D</span>
  <span id="freeText6410293289309405327" style="display:none"><a href="http://www.youtube.com/watch?v=bHVDibABUGk" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <b>(Soundtrack for this review)</b>
</a><br /><br />Do you want to know what I don't give a shit about? This book. <br /><br />I made it a little more than a quarter through it (35 pages to be exact). I found the writing to be blah (to be fair, I think it is the translation), the type to be a bit too small and the font to be faintly illegible. I dreaded having to go on break and read more of this book, and did internal backflips when I noticed the a new Harpers just went on sale before I went on break and saw there was a new DFW story in it. I broke my irrational ban on reading newly published DFW work in order to not read this book. <br /><br />It's about drugs. Which is kind of a boring subject. Especially when it's about drugs and rock n' roll in the late 1960's / early 1970's. Yawn-ville. People from the hippie generation acting like drugged out assholes. Oh, so fucking counter-culture. Even if the book is in Japan, some assholes different country. There isn't even any kind of cultural difference because it's all part of the universal culture of idiocy. <br /><br />This book did make me a little sad that because of some misguided decisions that I don't know nearly enough to really comment on, a country with it's own culture went into a war, lost, were occupied by a vacuous country that exported the worst they had to offer and undermine future generations. <br /><br />I could have plowed through this book. <br /><br />Instead my disgust with the book made me remember this quote from one of the members of Born Against:<br /><br /><i>"FUCK ROCK. Fuck cigarettes and slicked back hair that ends in a pony tail and a high door price. Maturity and progression do not mean status quo music and ideals. Under the false pretense of reaching new audiences, former punk bands bring us closer to square one, throwing out the window the foundation of independance which took years to build. This band is an attempt to reinforce those quickly eroding foundations. Maybe these "rock" types don't see it this way, but speaking as someone who has never been lured by the slippery slope of psuedo concerned UPC code post hardcore bullshit, common sense tells me that no matter how many reggae songs you write about human rights, your audience will still "rock out" and drink wine coolers. Then they'll go home and fuck like dogs to your politically concerned kick ass tunes. THE MEDIUM IS THE MESSAGE!</i></span>
  <a data-text-id="6410293289309405327" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">
          

<tr class="no_border feedFooterReview" id="update_comment_stuff_Review117856672">
  <td colspan="2">

<div id="review_1224887255" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1224887255" />
    <a title="Sean Blake" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/25471005-sean-blake"><img alt="Sean Blake" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1528978476p2/25471005.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1224887255?book_show_action=true">Mar 11, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sean Blake" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sean Blake" href="/user/show/25471005-sean-blake">Sean Blake</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/25471005-sean-blake?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1224887255" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer192957018524897834">I'm sure we all go through these moments in life, where we end up in a dark pit of frustration and fear, wondering where we should be or go, what we should do, why we are here. We, whether religious or atheist, have our moments where we feel as if life is without meaning, that we are simply an overly self conscious mammal that walks, talks, breathes and finally dies without feeling like we have accomplished much in our short lives. These moments are brief, yet during these precise moments, they </span>
  <span id="freeText192957018524897834" style="display:none">I'm sure we all go through these moments in life, where we end up in a dark pit of frustration and fear, wondering where we should be or go, what we should do, why we are here. We, whether religious or atheist, have our moments where we feel as if life is without meaning, that we are simply an overly self conscious mammal that walks, talks, breathes and finally dies without feeling like we have accomplished much in our short lives. These moments are brief, yet during these precise moments, they feel unbearably long and rip into your soul with almost malicious intent with a goal of searching for something -- anything, which will help you breathe just that little bit more...<br /><br />A group of young students are locked in a whirlwind of sex and drugs to the psychedelic music of the counterculture era in the early seventies like The Doors, Jimi Hendrix, Pink Floyd and The Rolling Stones. All lead a nihilistic existence and Ryū Murakami writes with such intensive prose, beautiful in its darkness, dark in its beauty, where our narrator (a fictional Ryū Murakami as the character, also named Ryū) and his friends are stuck in a constant crop rotation of despair, hallucinations, paranoia, surrealism and misery.<br /><br />Ryū Murakami questions our existence through these characters, crushing their hopes of a meaningful life with violence, emotionless sex, heroin, cocaine, hallucinogens and every other mind or mood altering drug you can think of. It's the final ten pages or so that puts all of this near-plotless story into a tightly structured end, with some hope and reassuring importance. Despite what a lot of people say, regarding a few chapters detailing graphic sex and orgies, <i>Almost Transparent Blue</i> is a literary tour de force, written incredibly well with the finest details on everything in life, rain, cockroaches, lights, the sea, ashtrays, death, toilets, trains are all described with visually strong prose and insight.<br /><br />On top of all of this, <i>Almost Transparent Blue</i> is, most importantly, a truly beautiful postmodern novel, a novel of originality, intelligence and existential emotion.</span>
  <a data-text-id="192957018524897834" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1412373488" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1412373488" />
    <a title="Ana" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/39605220-ana"><img alt="Ana" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1563620297p2/39605220.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1412373488?book_show_action=true">Oct 09, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ana" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ana" href="/user/show/39605220-ana">Ana</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1412373488" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3188482700175383762">I loved and will always love every bit of this book even when the only sratch from it on my memory will be left by images, flashing lights and stomach-churning acts of pluckiness (but purposeful). I am actually dreadfully sad that this had to end, I could go on and on with these youngsters' intrepid, grisly life. It had a 'hard not to notice' scent of Bret Easton Ellis and Burroughs in it, constantly chasing the reader. The only difference is that this is more soaked with meanings than many thin</span>
  <span id="freeText3188482700175383762" style="display:none">I loved and will always love every bit of this book even when the only sratch from it on my memory will be left by images, flashing lights and stomach-churning acts of pluckiness (but purposeful). I am actually dreadfully sad that this had to end, I could go on and on with these youngsters' intrepid, grisly life. It had a 'hard not to notice' scent of Bret Easton Ellis and Burroughs in it, constantly chasing the reader. The only difference is that this is more soaked with meanings than many think (by only being the onlookers of a junkie's session) and mesmerizing pieces of what means to be drugged, depressed, not loved but looking for all of them at once without knowing how to approach any. This book is kind of a merged scream of helplessness like some sort of urge by a bunch of youngsters who just actually want to have fun and liberate themselves from their own problems with the use of ignorance or unconsciousness. After reading what would this be all about I just thought that this is some kind of a border between sexual complexes and irksome group... that would be it, without nothing more or less than an endless adventure that will leave no trace in my mind. That's why I want to count here some of the facts that prevented me from thinking the same after finishing it. To start with, the shakes that this book give to your mind and soul are indelible: how they are all together but still alone, how they can always count on each other even if the sense of death throughout the book will creep every single bone of yours. With the highly manipulation of one's ability for imaginary visualisation Ryu Murakami manages to bring the reader into the reel of the damned, meaning that wherever you are you are with them, wherever they are you feel how the walls just compress you into their comradely hug, while the drugs just bump into your skin without you even notice: the struggle, the joy, the importance of friendship where everything seems like melting away. You can just feel the flicker of the neon in your eyes, percolating your body with the infection that's so treacly when it doesn't remind you how small you are there... together but all alone. Imagine this power of yours touching every character one by one like a joint, but with another density and with another sort of pain at its means. Because this book has it all: callous true love, brutal sex, jealousy, chase of spirituality (e.g: Okinawa's covet of hearing Ryu again with his flute, with the aim of replacing the addiction by finding his lost self), desire, fights, friendship in the name of drugs and a guy that wants to see the life with another eyes (the writer). To continue with, next to the sense of death lays the incapacity of all of them to draw to a close their miserable condition... once the methamphetamine is over, when the bordeom finds its throne. No matter how much they want to find succour in one another they will never be able to change their character which is seriously lacking stability. That's why another layer that convinces the reader that they are not ordinary characters is their bipolarity, expressed poles apart but in the same place. By means of that they manage to give a slowly collapsing unity to the whole book... which is a masterpiece from my point of view. Like Ryu said: just like a doll, moving her body, her mouth... but maybe I am just a doll. You can call this a dark humour book, not because it has jokes in it but because the puzzle in which we are in that appears to be dislocated from the first thrust is funny from nature. I do not want to be what Umberto Eco called: 'blur' (you know... the one that always has something good to say and for whom all the books are the best books that he/she had ever read), but this book is something, a short unputdownable that has so much to say in few pages. A book that thrives in you with all the energy it has, boosting the reader to cogitate at low speed but with acuteness, trust, love all his/her values. I do recommend this book with all my heart and I will always remember it myself. Up to now this is the only book I will want to re-read just for the hell of fulfilling myself with all that this can give over and over again.</span>
  <a data-text-id="3188482700175383762" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

        <a title="Joselito Honestly and Brilliantly" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Joselito Honestly and Brilliantly" href="/user/show/2190636-joselito-honestly-and-brilliantly">Joselito Honestly and Brilliantly</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer395938593" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8755970220249134696">Time to get stoned! This novel can put you in a catatonic torpor, a drug-induced haze near the point where you are about to overdose and die.<br/><br/>First person narrative, here is someone named Ryu, not yet twenty, with his equally-young male and female buddies. Ryu is telling a story. You hear him, but do not really understand the story being told.   <span id="freeText8755970220249134696" style="display:none">Time to get stoned! This novel can put you in a catatonic torpor, a drug-induced haze near the point where you are about to overdose and die.<br /><br />First person narrative, here is someone named Ryu, not yet twenty, with his equally-young male and female buddies. Ryu is telling a story. You hear him, but do not really understand the story being told. The images will distract you and catch your full attention.<br /><br />First there's this cockroach on some unwashed dishes. Then it is squashed. See the jelly-like substance that spurts from its violated body. And then the old, cut pineapple, its rancid odor; the blood on needles, heroin, hashish, mescaline, Nibrole pills dissolved in gin, Philopon, shooting up, vomit, spittle, saliva, unwashed smelly bodies, rock music, The Doors, Rolling Stones, Mal Waldron, James Brown, Billy Holiday, mouldy soup, rotten bean curd, dirty rooms, leftover roast chicken gone completely bad, old radio, black American studs with huge dicks, Japanese girls with small pussies, sex orgies--<br /><br /><br />"Pressing her chin on the table, breathin hard, Moko attacked a crab like a starving child. Then one of the blacks stuck his shaft in front of her, and she took that in her mouth too. Stroking it with her tongue, she pushed it aside and turned again to the crab. The red shell crunched between her teeth, she pulled out the white meat with her hands. Piling it with pink mayonnaise from a plate, she put it on her tongue, the mayonnaise dribbling onto her chest. The odor of crab flowed through the room. On the bed, Reiko was still howling. Durham pushed up into Moko from behind. Her butt jiggled, she held onto the crab, her face twisted, she tried to drink some wine but with the rocking of her body it went into her nose and she choked, tears in her eyes. Seeing that, Kei laughed loudly. James Brown began to sing...."<br /><br />On to the glorious ending, a dead moth, a crazed Ryu ingesting insects, contemplates on a dead moth, sees a huge black bird, hallucinates, stabs his arm with a broken glass, bleeds and bleeds until he becomes "a boundless blue, almost transparent."<br /><br />He survives, but only to write this novel.</span>

<div id="review_60017032" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/60017032" />
    <a title="Chloe" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/70078-chloe"><img alt="Chloe" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1580564408p2/70078.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/60017032?book_show_action=true">Jun 17, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Chloe" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Chloe" href="/user/show/70078-chloe">Chloe</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Junkies, Losers and Burnouts</span></div>

          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Chloe by: </span>
      Matt Darnell

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer60017032" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7471075118339612434">Those who know me are well aware that I have an almost visceral love of twisted literature. Books that are the literary equivalent of a car crash; hedonistic revels that fade into subterranean nightmares. In other words, such extreme excess that you can't help but approach them with a mixture of sick curiosity and nearly overwhelming trepidation. For the longest time that has meant the blasted imaginations of my trifecta of favorite authors (Palahniuk, Irvine Welsh and Bret Easton Ellis) and occ</span>
  <span id="freeText7471075118339612434" style="display:none">Those who know me are well aware that I have an almost visceral love of twisted literature. Books that are the literary equivalent of a car crash; hedonistic revels that fade into subterranean nightmares. In other words, such extreme excess that you can't help but approach them with a mixture of sick curiosity and nearly overwhelming trepidation. For the longest time that has meant the blasted imaginations of my trifecta of favorite authors (Palahniuk, Irvine Welsh and Bret Easton Ellis) and occasional one-offs by up-and-coming writers such as Jeremy Robert Johnson's <i>Angel Dust Apocalypse</i>. Very rarely do I stumble across an author with a well-established body of work of the sort that I like to mainline like a junkie fresh from rehab.<br /><br />Which is why I'm more than a little excited to have been exposed to Ryu Murakami. Within the first three pages of his Akutagama-winning debut novel, Murakami's disaffected Japanese youth growing up in the shadow of an American military base huff glue, shoot up some heroin and have sex in a flophouse that Burroughs would feel at home in. The next 100-odd pages are packed to the brim with more of the same- violent orgies with American servicemen, madcap mescaline adventures that end with them crashing a car onto a runway, more heroin, flashmobs beating security guards senseless and other signs of impending armageddon. <br /><br />Murakami's plotless (pointless?) novella pinballs between the restless ennui of Bret Easton Ellis' <i>Less Than Zero</i> and the senseless violence of Anthony Burgess' <i>A Clockwork Orange</i>. At times extremely disturbing (I could have gone my whole life without reading the scene with the foot), Murakami still manages to paste together an interesting tale of cultureless youth emulating what they see as a stronger people, the servicemen who blow a mean sax, have the best drugs, and that suave intoxicating confidence of knowing you can do no wrong. A phenomenally quick read, not for the faint of heart, that has me excited to finally get to his more well-known books such as <i>In The Miso Soup</i> and <i>Coin Locker Babies</i>.</span>
  <a data-text-id="7471075118339612434" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_453445526" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/453445526" />
    <a title="Mike" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2201886-mike"><img alt="Mike" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1239392797p2/2201886.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/453445526?book_show_action=true">Nov 11, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Mike" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Mike" href="/user/show/2201886-mike">Mike</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Anyone</span></div>

        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer453445526" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14359689345819693053">As soon as I had read the first page, I knew that I had read “Almost Transparent Blue” at least once many years ago. Even though the weighty & lengthy TBR shelf chides me, I read it through on a long train ride. And I am glad I did. I know like and appreciate this more more than I did before.<br/><br/>This is Murakami-san’s first novel written in his youth about even younger youths. Is it autobiographical? I don’t know and don’t recall anyone ever saying so definitively. The main character shares his pers</span>
  <span id="freeText14359689345819693053" style="display:none">As soon as I had read the first page, I knew that I had read “Almost Transparent Blue” at least once many years ago. Even though the weighty &amp; lengthy TBR shelf chides me, I read it through on a long train ride. And I am glad I did. I know like and appreciate this more more than I did before.<br /><br />This is Murakami-san’s first novel written in his youth about even younger youths. Is it autobiographical? I don’t know and don’t recall anyone ever saying so definitively. The main character shares his personal name “Ryu” and certain places and situations reflect locations that the author was exposed to in these and earlier years.<br /><br />You could call the novel “decadent”. You could also call it “self-indulgent” (“Ryu” and his friends are very much so). And despite it not having a traditional plot, I think that the layout of the novel and its progression was worked out either during the writing of the novel or during his editing (if any). Because with this second (third possibly?) reading I was able to see more of a pattern to it. But it feels like a stream-of-consciousness account of several days in the life of “Ryu”. How consecutive these days are or are not is difficult to discern.<br /><br />Was it written as a form or rebellion against the status quo? Probably. In the 70s in Japan, as well as the US, the counter-culture that took root at the end of the 60s was still in full swing. The author himself participated in similar things as what goes on in the book. No matter how much we in the US think that society and family conspire to control how we behave and interact (I am taking the hard view here), it is nothing compared to those pressures in Japan and many other Asian countries.<br /><br />The phrase, “the nail that sticks up will be hammered down” is a true insight into how conformity has been the overwhelming force in Japanese society for hundreds of years. A small island with a large population and few natural resources, they exist in population concentrations that we seldom encounter. Thus, cooperation and conformity are almost a necessity (again I exaggerate). Under such “rules” there is always a backlash. And in Japan that backlash has and I think always will be spectacular.<br /><br />Many of my friends in Japan are non-traditional. Some more so than others, of course and none as wild as those in the novel. But, they have a spark in them that connects back to the rebellion that is in these characters. Asian cultures are often much more tolerant of the misdeeds of their children up until their twenties. But when they reach a certain age, then they are expected to transition into the role of good workers, citizens, and members of society. I’ve seen people who have done exactly that and others who reversed course and took themselves further out of play.<br /><br />At the end of this novel, we are left to wonder what will happen to most of the players. But one, and perhaps the most surprising one, peels away and is not heard from again. Have they made their leap into conformity? We are never sure. But it is implied.<br /><br />(Note there is another character who tells “Ryu” that this is almost exactly her own plan. Once she is bored with all of this (i.e. the drugs, sex, and rock n’ roll) she will settle down and get married.)<br /><br />This is a stupendous first book by an author who went on to pen other works that have also been critically (and popularly) acclaimed. It can easily turn many people off, but it is different, strong, and entertaining. Four (4) star minimum – maybe “4.5”.<br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="14359689345819693053" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText3178244347608814264" style="display:none">So, a tale of Japanese junkies shooting up, having sex, slitting wrists, crushing insects and vomiting a lot. I guess there is some symbolic stuff going on, that if I was smarter or could be bothered, I could piece together. But to my addled eyes, it reads as what it is, a first novel by a 20-something literary talent out to shock stretching his wings for a maiden flight. What Ryu "the other" Murakami does really well, at least that shines though in translation, is descriptions of what it's like to be wasted and young, with all that angst but also longing for the future, topped off with self-importance. And body fluids. But he could do with bit more of a plot and it would be nifty if he could write something that wasn't thinly veiled embellished autobiography. But, for a first effort, pretty good, if gross and un-engrossing for the middle-aged reader like me.<br /><br />Download my starter library for free here - <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://eepurl.com/bFkt0X">http://eepurl.com/bFkt0X</a> - and receive my monthly newsletter with an original painting, and book recommendations galore for the Japanophile, crime-fiction-lover in all of us.</span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1626202204" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1626202204" />
    <a title="Shaun" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2621599-shaun"><img alt="Shaun" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1304973778p2/2621599.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1626202204?book_show_action=true">Jun 02, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Shaun" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Shaun" href="/user/show/2621599-shaun">Shaun</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780870113055" href="/book/show/533785.Almost_Transparent_Blue">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2621599-shaun?shelf=modern-literature-existentialism">modern-literature-existentialism</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2621599-shaun?shelf=1001-books-to-read-before-dying">1001-books-to-read-before-dying</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1626202204" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13247810088001829065">What can be said about this peculiar little book that has not already been said by far more intelligent folks than your's truly?! Yes. It's a quick read. Yes. It's gritty with the details of drug abuse and sexual orgies "[r]endered in excruciating, often repugnant detail." If you don't like either drug abuse and/or sexual conduct given in such "repugnant detail", don't read it. If, however, you want an introduction to one of Japan's finest, post-modernism writers, then DO read this book. <br/><br/>I am go</span>
  <span id="freeText13247810088001829065" style="display:none">What can be said about this peculiar little book that has not already been said by far more intelligent folks than your's truly?! Yes. It's a quick read. Yes. It's gritty with the details of drug abuse and sexual orgies "[r]endered in excruciating, often repugnant detail." If you don't like either drug abuse and/or sexual conduct given in such "repugnant detail", don't read it. If, however, you want an introduction to one of Japan's finest, post-modernism writers, then DO read this book. <br /><br />I am going to quote heavily from University of Oxford doctoral candidate Bianca Jackson from Peter Boxall's amazing little big book, "1001 Books You Must Read Before You Die" (2010 ed) because it is so right on and righteous here.<br /><br />"Ryu Murakami's 'Almost Transparent Blue' describes the day-to-day existence of a group of nihilistic [and morally challenged] youths living near an American army base in an unnamed Japanese port town. The narrator, Ryu, and his friends have rejected the formulaic lifestyle of the 'straights' -- including job security, family, and most importantly, moral constraints. Rather, they devote their shared existence to drugs, sexual orgies, and music. Seemingly without a plot, the novel outlines the boredom, alienation, and subsequent depravity of a generation lacking purpose."<br /><br />Whew! That's calling it like it is. Even now some 40 years later nihilism and immorality seems to be running rampant. The questions arises: Why? Why have things gotten so far down the drain? Where, when and how did we lose our humanity? Was it during the Sixties? Or did it occur sooner during the Fifties? Forties? Thirties? Twenties? Or Teens of the early 20th Century right around the First World War?<br /><br />Okay, okay, I know I mentioned this one earlier, but I think a bit of Fernando Pessoa's "The Book of Disquiet" originally written around 1931 to 1934 but finally published in 1982 may help provide the answer: <br /><br />"Art gives us the illusion or liberation from the sordid business of being. Whilst (dig that word!) feeling the slings and arrows of Hamlet, prince of Denmark, we do not feel our own, which are base because they are ours and because they are in themselves base." Huh? A bit of a tautological reasoning there. <br /><br />But the Maestro goes on and states, "Love, sleep, drugs and intoxicants are elementary forms of art or rather elementary forms of producing the same effect as art. But love, sleep, and drugs all bring with them their own disappointments. One grows sated or disillusioned with love. We wake from sleep and whilst (there it is, again!) we slept, we did not live. The price of drugs is the ruin of every body they were used to stimulate. But there is no disillusion in art because its illusory nature is clear from the start. One does not wake from art because, although we dream it, we are not asleep. There is no tribute or fine to pay for having enjoyed art. Since in some way it is not ours, we do not have to pay for or repent from the pleasure it offers us. ... To possess something is to lose it. To feel something without possessing it is to keep it, because in that way one extracts its essence." No. 256 [518] from "The Book of Disquiet" (Serpent's Tail Publishers) (1991, 2010 ed). Now back to Bianca.<br /><br />"Written when he was only twenty-three, Ryu Murakami's 'Almost Transparent Blue' refuses to spare the reader's sensibilities. The audience is forced to partake in a catalog of graphic sexual violence and drug-induced frenzies, unable to avert its gaze from the unpalatable. But beneath the meticulous descriptions of the grotesque is a stark portrait of a universal loneliness, reminiscent of Albert Camus or Franz Kafka. Straying away from the introspective trend of postwar literature, this enfant terrible of the Japanese art world (I presume she means Ryu here and not just this book itself) annihilates the Japan of snowcapped mountains and cherry blossoms, exposing the underbelly of a culture in flux. Dividing readers and critics alike, 'Almost Transparent Blue' won the prestigious Akutagawa Literary Prize in 1976, and became an instant bestseller." <br /><br />Personally, an earlier Japanese author -- Yukio Mishima -- was "[s]traying away from the introspective trend of postwar literature" annihilating those precious Japanese "snowcapped mountains and cherry blossoms [and] exposing the underbelly of a culture in flux" in such works as "The Sound of Waves" and his four (4) volume work "The Sea of Fertility". But there's no denying this little beauty does it far less subtlety and far more crudely "in your face" than does Mishima's work.<br /><br />Time to avoid considering "the slings and arrows" of my own base nature and go engage in more coolish escapism reading some Russian existentialism and post-modernism literature. Yes, 'tis a rockin' good reading life after all!</span>
  <a data-text-id="13247810088001829065" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer453301993" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5242467295676071441">It will take a bit of digestion to form a coherent response to this book. The first half was a drag. Violent sex and drug use depicted with skilled, lovely prose, a special focus on body fluids. The only factors that kept me reading were the shortness of the book and the author's skill, because these first sixty pages were deathly boring.<br/><br/>In the second half, something like a story of a dark personal transformation begins to emerge. Free of the blizzard of pills and orgies, the writing focuses on </span>
  <span id="freeText5242467295676071441" style="display:none">It will take a bit of digestion to form a coherent response to this book. The first half was a drag. Violent sex and drug use depicted with skilled, lovely prose, a special focus on body fluids. The only factors that kept me reading were the shortness of the book and the author's skill, because these first sixty pages were deathly boring.<br /><br />In the second half, something like a story of a dark personal transformation begins to emerge. Free of the blizzard of pills and orgies, the writing focuses on extraordinary, aesthetic images. A field of tomatoes, a dead moth, the night sky, a blood-smeared shard of glass all reveal hidden beauty and strange connection. This half of the book is wondrous enough to justify reading the entire novel itself.<br /><br />Additionally, I suspect the author is doing something or has a message. It might be profound. It's probably at least interesting. It's not forthcoming. The book offers a couple easy ways to interpret it through an appended paragraph at the very end, but I get the feeling more is moving beneath the surface.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5242467295676071441" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_314850291" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/314850291" />
    <a title="Erik F." class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7324260-erik-f"><img alt="Erik F." src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1346629842p2/7324260.jpg" /></a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer314850291" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18444116517120567601">I don’t have much to say about this brief and rather shallow debut, which details the squalid sex-and-drug-fueled existences of a group of young Japanese lowlifes/layabouts in the 1970s, except that it’s lurid, grotesque, delirious, disturbing, and oddly compelling. (I say “compelling,” but my readerly propulsion through the book may simply have been my desire to escape from these sordid characters and their dead-end lives as soon as possible). I wouldn’t recommend this book to anyone or ever re</span>
  <span id="freeText18444116517120567601" style="display:none">I don’t have much to say about this brief and rather shallow debut, which details the squalid sex-and-drug-fueled existences of a group of young Japanese lowlifes/layabouts in the 1970s, except that it’s lurid, grotesque, delirious, disturbing, and oddly compelling. (I say “compelling,” but my readerly propulsion through the book may simply have been my desire to escape from these sordid characters and their dead-end lives as soon as possible). I wouldn’t recommend this book to anyone or ever read it again, but it has a scorching power that’s impossible to deny, radiating moments of strange, sensuous, shocking beauty amidst the filth and fury.</span>

<tr class="no_border feedFooterReview" id="update_comment_stuff_Review314850291">

<div id="review_420022561" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/420022561" />
    <a title="Toby" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5454475-toby"><img alt="Toby" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1542247337p2/5454475.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/420022561?book_show_action=true">Sep 22, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Toby" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Toby" href="/user/show/5454475-toby">Toby</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5454475-toby?shelf=translation">translation</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5454475-toby?shelf=lit">lit</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer420022561" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8815221639591868439"><b>Female News Host #1: On a personal note, that shit was fucked up!<br/>News Co-Host #1: Yeah it was. I literally pissed myself.</b><br/><br/>Those words may well be from an entirely different medium but they essentially capture the blasé attitude of the characters in Ryu Murakami's debut novel. And on a personal note, that shit really was fucked up!<br/><br/>A plotless look at the lives of a group of friends who have partied way too hard for way too long and are now living a destructive lifestyle but don't really seem to car</span>
  <span id="freeText8815221639591868439" style="display:none"><b>Female News Host #1: On a personal note, that shit was fucked up!<br />News Co-Host #1: Yeah it was. I literally pissed myself.</b><br /><br />Those words may well be from an entirely different medium but they essentially capture the blasé attitude of the characters in Ryu Murakami's debut novel. And on a personal note, that shit really was fucked up!<br /><br />A plotless look at the lives of a group of friends who have partied way too hard for way too long and are now living a destructive lifestyle but don't really seem to care what happens one way or another.<br /><br /><i>
  <blockquote>I was completely stoned. I felt as if my insides were oozing out through every pore, and other people's sweat and breath were flowing in. Especially the lower half of my body felt heavy and sore, as if sunk into thick mud, and my mouth itched to hold somebody's prick and drain it. While we ate the fruit piled on plates and drank wine, the whole room was raped with heat. I wanted my skin peeled off. I wanted to take in the greased, shiny bodies of the black men and rock them inside me. Cherry cheesecake, grapes in black hands, steaming boiled crab legs breaking with a snap, clear sweet pale purple American wine, pickles like dead men's wart-covered fingers, bacon sandwiches like the mouths of women, salad dripping pink mayonnaise.</blockquote>
</i><br /><br />That is essentially the first half of this novel captured in one paragraph. I wouldn't say that I am a prude or generally squeemish when it comes to literature; I have thoroughly enjoyed other novels from this author, especially <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/818118.In_The_Miso_Soup" title="In The Miso Soup by Ryū Murakami" rel="nofollow noopener">In The Miso Soup</a>, but this was a very difficult read. Four days to read a 125 page novel? There were times when I ust couldn't face reading more than two or three pages at any one time. But that doesn't necessarily mean it was bad, just that this Murakami doesn't write things that can be inhumed without serious consideration of its content.<br /><br />As the first half orgy ends the book becomes an easier read, I found myself enjoying it a lot more, if you can call a novel that features random acts of violence, suicide attempts, copious amounts of illegal and hallucinogenic substances and rambling incoherent thoughts enjoyable. Murakami creates images like no other author I've experienced and in this case it saved the novel for me.<br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="8815221639591868439" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1786328902" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1786328902" />
    <a title="Griffin Alexander" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/25982974-griffin-alexander"><img alt="Griffin Alexander" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1522817332p2/25982974.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1786328902?book_show_action=true">Oct 17, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Griffin Alexander" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Griffin Alexander" href="/user/show/25982974-griffin-alexander">Griffin Alexander</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1786328902" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12849754876446049089">A bleak portrayal of a defeated country's youth culture that both consumes American (counter-) culture and is consumed by it. In defiance against the established order of madness that led Japan to a Pyrrhic defeat at the hands of the Allies, the youth here are afloat in their own existence against their cultural inheritance, but with no substantive alternative—suicide here is merely gestural and holds none of the symbolic or existential weight that it is given by the likes of Mishima/Kawabata/Ak</span>
  <span id="freeText12849754876446049089" style="display:none">A bleak portrayal of a defeated country's youth culture that both consumes American (counter-) culture and is consumed by it. In defiance against the established order of madness that led Japan to a Pyrrhic defeat at the hands of the Allies, the youth here are afloat in their own existence against their cultural inheritance, but with no substantive alternative—suicide here is merely gestural and holds none of the symbolic or existential weight that it is given by the likes of Mishima/Kawabata/Akutagawa etc. in Japan's literary past. American imports stand in as the sole source of cultural interest and also source of extreme indifference as records interchangeably grace the turntable to little effect upon the listeners aside from demarcating the time expended and the names of various bands rattled off from LP covers.<br /><br />Despite the criticisms levied against it, this novel is not plotless—it is too short for that. There is a good deal of drinking/heroin/hash, a pretty sad drug/sex party with some GIs in which our narrator is sexually imposed upon (metaphorically and otherwise), a good long mescaline trip w/ revelations concerning the US military's colonization of Japanese consciousness, and finally a rock n roll concert where our ragtag group of junkie freaks beat the shit out of a guard—all leading to the disillusionment of the narrator and his self-harm and subsequent self-removal from this strange (and somehow very compelling) cycle of events. The chief reveal of this semi-cyclical structure is its portrayal of the willing subjection of youth to the project of absence sought in the transcendent painfulness of rough sex and heavy drug use. It is not a true desire for absence (as per the note on suicide above), but rather a desire for passive objectness, that is, what goes into both existing and interacting (even if that means being used by others in way that disregards your very existence as another and equal <i>subject</i>) without the need to make decisions, or inhabit action mindfully. <br /><br />Though this review may make <i>ATB</i> come off as a simple novelistic depiction of postwar anomie, Murakami actually makes it all rather compelling, surprisingly erotic (albeit with sublimation [when it happens] merely pointing away <i>from</i> boredom/ennui, rather than <i>toward</i> anything productive), and simultaneously disquieting—it is an interesting and uncommon trio to experience all at once. There is a certain pathos present that is missing from the more famous western examples like <i>Train Spotting</i> or <i>Naked Lunch</i>. Here the action never reaches the pitch of black humor or tongue-in-cheek proto-sci-fi-hallucinatory-commentary; rather, we simply have what is, this object of text, and that is perhaps what feels the strangest.</span>
  <a data-text-id="12849754876446049089" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3289284015" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3289284015" />
    <a title="Jovana Autumn" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/41176805-jovana-autumn"><img alt="Jovana Autumn" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1579813627p2/41176805.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3289284015?book_show_action=true">Apr 20, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jovana Autumn" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jovana Autumn" href="/user/show/41176805-jovana-autumn">Jovana Autumn</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/41176805-jovana-autumn?shelf=my-read-ebooks">my-read-ebooks</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/41176805-jovana-autumn?shelf=2020">2020</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/41176805-jovana-autumn?shelf=reviews">reviews</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/41176805-jovana-autumn?shelf=read-in-serbian">read-in-serbian</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/41176805-jovana-autumn?shelf=japanese-writers">japanese-writers</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3289284015" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7858629489175249640">Okay, this book delivers just what it says in the blurb.<br/><br/>We follow a gang of young Japanese men and women and their seemingly empty lives filled with drug abuse, alcohol abuse, and orgies. <br/><br/><i>There’s nothing really happening here. </i><br/><br/>All of them have bad coping mechanisms with life, Ryu (the character) contemplates life the most in this novel. <br/><br/>Don’t get me wrong, the characters are beyond likable, all they think about is what to do next to bring them a bit of excitement in their lives – like partying, </span>
  <span id="freeText7858629489175249640" style="display:none">Okay, this book delivers just what it says in the blurb.<br /><br />We follow a gang of young Japanese men and women and their seemingly empty lives filled with drug abuse, alcohol abuse, and orgies. <br /><br /><i>There’s nothing really happening here. </i><br /><br />All of them have bad coping mechanisms with life, Ryu (the character) contemplates life the most in this novel. <br /><br />Don’t get me wrong, the characters are beyond likable, all they think about is what to do next to bring them a bit of excitement in their lives – like partying, sex or more drugs. Their lives are pretty much non-existent outside of that.<br /><br />And who knew, guess Ryu Murakami isn’t a shit writer, after all, I actually got into the book and was disgusted by a couple of scenes and I had the interest to see what will happen to these characters. So kudos for actually making me feel something out of reading the book.<br /><br /><b>
  <i>Now, after reading the book will you get anything out of it? Well, yes and no.</i>
</b><br /><br />Yes in a sense you will be close to seeing the mind of addicts and people who find comfort in substance abuse – which isn’t pretty at all. These people need help and nobody is providing them with it.<br />And No, there isn’t really a point of the book besides that.<br /><br />Still not something that I will really think about in the future, but it is definitely more memorable than <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/17810.In_the_Miso_Soup" title="In the Miso Soup by Ryū Murakami" rel="nofollow noopener">In the Miso Soup</a> and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/3392668.Audition" title="Audition by Ryū Murakami" rel="nofollow noopener">Audition</a>. <br />-------------------------------------------------------------<br />Could this be the only Ryu Murakami Book that I actually didn't mind? RTC.</span>
  <a data-text-id="7858629489175249640" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
